But the three smallest divisors greater than 1 are d_2, d_3, d_4, where d_2 is the smallest divisor >1 (i.e., p), and d_3 and d_4 are the next smallest divisors >1, which could be p^2, q, etc., but they must be less than N, because N is a divisor, but it is the largest divisor; however, we only consider divisors less than N? Actually when we say the three smallest divisors greater than 1, we are looking at the set of all divisors of N that are >1, including N itself? But N is a divisor and it is >1, but it is the largest divisor. However, the three smallest divisors >1 are the three smallest elements of the set of divisors greater than 1. That set includes 1? No, we exclude 1. So the smallest divisor >1 is p, then the next smallest divisor >1 could be either p^2 if p^2 divides N, or another prime q if q divides N, etc. But note that N itself is a divisor, but it is the largest, so it will not be among the three smallest unless N is very small. For N>4, the three smallest divisors >1 are all less than N. For N=6: divisors>1: 2,3,6. So the three smallest are 2,3,6. But then d_2=2, d_3=3, d_4=6. But 6 is N, and it's a divisor >1; however, we need to be careful: In the definition of S(N) we use the three largest proper divisors, which are N/d_2, N/d_3, N/d_4. For N=6, d_2=2 => N/d_2=3; d_3=3 => N/d_3=2; d_4=6 => N/d_4=1. So the three largest proper divisors are 3,2,1. That's consistent. So d_4 can be N itself (if N has exactly three divisors >1). But then N/d_4=1, which is a proper divisor. So it's fine.

Thus in the equation 1/p + 1/d_3 + 1/d_4 = 1, we need to consider d_3 and d_4 as the next smallest divisors >1, and they can be N itself if N has exactly three divisors >1? Actually if N has exactly three divisors >1, then d_2, d_3, d_4 are the three smallest divisors >1, and d_4 = N. So d_4 = N. Then 1/p + 1/d_3 + 1/N = 1. Since N>d_3, 1/N is small. But we can consider that case.

But more generally, we want to characterize numbers N such that sum of three largest proper divisors = N. That is the fixed point condition.

Let's find all N satisfying S(N)=N.

Let p be smallest prime factor. Let the three smallest divisors >1 be p, d, e (with p < d < e, and e could be N). Then S(N)=N/p + N/d + N/e = N => 1/p+1/d+1/e=1.

We need to find all positive integer triples (p,d,e) with p prime, d,e >1, p < d < e, such that 1/p+1/d+1/e=1, and then N must be such that the three smallest divisors >1 are exactly p,d,e (in that order), and also N must be composite with at least three proper divisors, which it will be.

First, solve the diophantine equation.

Case p=2: Then 1/2+1/d+1/e=1 => 1/d+1/e=1/2 => (d+e)/(de)=1/2 => 2(d+e)=de => de-2d-2e=0 => (d-2)(e-2)=4. So we need positive integers d,e >2? Since p=2, d>2? Actually d > p=2, so d>=3, e>d. Then (d-2)(e-2)=4. Factor pairs of 4: (1,4),(2,2),(4,1). So:
- (d-2)=1, (e-2)=4 => d=3, e=6.
- (d-2)=2, (e-2)=2 => d=4, e=4. But d<e required, so not allowed.
- (d-2)=4, (e-2)=1 => d=6, e=3. Not allowed since d<e.
Thus only solution: d=3, e=6.

So (p,d,e)=(2,3,6). That means the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6. But note that 6 is N itself? Since e=6, and e is the third smallest divisor >1, so N must be such that its divisors >1 are exactly 2,3,6 and maybe others? Actually if the three smallest divisors >1 are 2,3,6, then N must have at least these divisors, and 6 is the largest among these three. But N itself is a divisor; if N=6, then the divisors >1 are 2,3,6. So N=6 works. Could N be larger? If N has divisors >1 including 2,3,6, but also other divisors that are less than 6? No, because 2 and 3 are the smallest; 6 is the third smallest. If N has other divisors, they would be either >6 or between 3 and 6. Could there be a divisor between 3 and 6? The only integer between 3 and 6 is 4 and 5. So if N has 4 as a divisor, then 4 would be a divisor between 3 and 6, making the third smallest divisor 4, not 6. So N cannot have 4 or 5 as divisors. However, if N>6 and has a divisor d with 3<d<6, that would be a problem. But can N have a divisor 4? That would require N divisible by 4. But if N is divisible by 4, then since p=2, N is even. If N is divisible by 4, then 4 is a divisor. So then the third smallest divisor would be 4, not 6. So N cannot be divisible by 4. Similarly, cannot be divisible by 5. Similarly, N=54: divisors:1,2,3,6,9,18,27,54. Smallest >1:2,3,6. So p=2,d=3,e=6. S(54)=54/2+54/3+54/6=27+18+9=54. So N=54 works. N=66: divisors:1,2,3,6,11,22,33,66. Smallest:2,3,6. S=33+22+11=66. So works. N=78: divisors:1,2,3,6,13,26,39,78. Smallest:2,3,6. S=39+26+13=78. So works. So all numbers that are multiples of 6 but not divisible by 4 or 5? Wait, check 12: divisors:1,2,3,4,6,12. So the condition for a number N to be a fixed point is that the three smallest divisors >1 are exactly 2,3,6. That implies:
- N is even (so p=2)
- N is divisible by 3 (so d=3)
- N is divisible by 6 (so e=6 is a divisor)
- N is NOT divisible by 4 (since otherwise 4 would be a divisor between 3 and 6)
- N is NOT divisible by 5 (since 5 would be a divisor between 3 and 6)
But also need that there is no divisor between 3 and 6 other than 4,5? 4 and 5 are the only integers between 3 and 6. But also note that N=6 is the smallest fixed point. Also what about numbers that are multiples of 6 but also divisible by 4 or 5? They are not fixed points.

Thus the set of fixed points is: F = { N ∈ ℕ : N ≡ 6 (mod 12) and 5 ∤ N }.

Now, any a1 in F yields constant sequence, so works.

What about numbers that map to F in one step? For a1 = 72, we got a2=78 ∈ F. So 72 works. Are there numbers that map to F in two steps? For instance, a1 such that a2 = some number that maps to F? But then a2 must have at least 3 proper divisors and S(a2) ∈ F. So we need to find all numbers N such that S(N) is a fixed point. Those N would be "preimages" of fixed points. They could be valid a1 if S(N) is in F and N itself has at least 3 proper divisors and all subsequent terms are good (i.e., S(N) is fixed point, so constant). But we also need that N itself has at least three proper divisors, which is given. So we need to find all numbers N such that S(N) is in F, and also that N has at least three proper divisors (so N is composite and not a prime or prime square). Additionally, we need that S(N) is not prime or prime square? But if S(N) ∈ F, then it's a fixed point, so it's composite and has at least three proper divisors (since F numbers are composite with at least 3 proper divisors). So the sequence from N will be: N -> M (where M = S(N)) -> M -> ... So it's defined infinitely. So any such N is a valid a1.

Thus the problem reduces to: Determine all positive integers a1 such that iterating the function S (sum of three largest proper divisors) eventually reaches a number in F, and all intermediate terms have at least three proper divisors. But also note: Could there be numbers that never reach F but instead enter a cycle of length >1? That would also be infinite, but we need to check if such cycles exist. We should analyze the behavior of S.

First, let's characterize S(N) in terms of N and its divisors.

Let N be composite with at least three proper divisors. Let p be smallest prime factor. Then the three largest proper divisors are: N/p (largest proper divisor), N/d (second largest, where d is the second smallest divisor >1), and N/e (third largest, where e is the third smallest divisor >1). So S(N) = N/p + N/d + N/e = N * (1/p + 1/d + 1/e). Note that p,d,e are the three smallest divisors >1, with p < d < e (if e is N, then e = N, but then N/e =1, which is allowed). So we have S(N) = N * T, where T = 1/p + 1/d + 1/e.

We need to understand possible values of T. Since p,d,e are divisors >1, and p is the smallest prime factor, T is a rational number less than 1? Actually S(N) is the sum of three proper divisors, which is less than N? Not necessarily; from examples, S(6)=6, S(12)=13>12, S(18)=18, S(30)=31>30, S(42)=42, S(54)=54, S(66)=66, S(78)=78, S(90)=93>90, S(96)=104>96, S(100)=95<100. So S(N) can be less than, equal to, or greater than N. So T can be <1, =1, or >1.

We want to find all a1 such that the sequence is defined infinitely. That is, all terms are composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Since the sequence might become unbounded? Possibly, but we need to ensure it never hits a number with fewer than 3 proper divisors. That's a kind of "good" set.

We should try to characterize the set of numbers that can appear in such a sequence. This is reminiscent of problems where the function is defined as the sum of the largest proper divisors, and the only possible values are those where the number is "3-perfect" or something. Perhaps the only numbers that can appear in the sequence are those that are multiples of 6? But we saw 12 fails because S(12)=13 prime, so 12 cannot be in the sequence because if a_n =12, then a_{n+1}=13, which fails. However, a1 could be 72, which is multiple of 6? 72/6=12, so 72 is a multiple of 6, but 72 itself is not in F? Actually 72 is not in F because 72 is divisible by 4? 72 mod 12 =0, so divisible by 4. So 72 is not a fixed point, but it maps to 78 which is in F. So 72 appears in a sequence. So numbers that are not in F can appear as long as they map to a good number.

But note: For a sequence to be infinite, all terms must have at least three proper divisors. So if we have a number N that maps to a number that eventually fails (like a prime), then N cannot be a valid a1. So we need to find all numbers that are "good" under iteration.

We can approach by analyzing the function S(N) in terms of prime factorization. Let's attempt to find all N such that S(N) is composite with at least three proper divisors. That is, we want to avoid S(N) being prime or prime square (since those have only 1 or 2 proper divisors). Actually prime has only proper divisor 1; prime square has proper divisors 1 and p, so two proper divisors. So we need S(N) to have at least 3 proper divisors, i.e., tau(S(N)) >= 4.

Thus we need to characterize N such that S(N) is not prime and not a prime square. This is a necessary condition for the sequence to continue. But also we need that S(N) itself has at least 3 proper divisors, which is the same condition. So we need to find all N such that the iteration stays in the set of numbers with tau >= 4 (i.e., composite and not prime power with exponent 1 or 2). Actually numbers with tau >= 4 include prime powers p^3 and higher, and numbers with at least two distinct primes. So we need to avoid numbers that are prime or prime square.

We can try to analyze the structure of S(N). Let p be smallest prime factor. Then S(N) = N/p + N/d + N/e. Since d and e are divisors, N/d and N/e are integers. So S(N) is an integer. Also note that N/p is the largest proper divisor, so S(N) > N/p. Also S(N) could be larger than N.

Let's try to find all N such that S(N) = N (fixed points). Already characterized: N divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5. So F = { N : N ≡ 6 mod 12 and 5 ∤ N }.

Now, consider N such that S(N) is a fixed point. That is, S(N) ∈ F. Then we have S(S(N)) = S(N) because fixed point. So such N are "preimages" of F. Are there any other cycles? Could there be a 2-cycle where N and M are distinct and S(N)=M, S(M)=N? For such a 2-cycle, we need S(N)=M and S(M)=N. Since both N and M are composite with at least 3 proper divisors. Let's attempt to find such numbers.

Suppose N and M are distinct and S(N)=M, S(M)=N. Then consider the equation: N = S(M) = M/p_M + M/d_M + M/e_M, and M = S(N) = N/p_N + N/d_N + N/e_N. So we have N = M * T_M, M = N * T_N, where T_M = 1/p_M + 1/d_M + 1/e_M, T_N = 1/p_N + 1/d_N + 1/e_N. Then N = M * T_M = (N * T_N) * T_M => 1 = T_N * T_M. So T_N * T_M = 1. Since T_N and T_M are rational numbers >0, and both are sums of reciprocals of divisors, they are at most? T_N could be >1 or <1. But if T_N * T_M = 1, then either both are 1 (giving fixed point) or one is >1 and the other <1. So we need T_N > 1 and T_M < 1 (or vice versa). T_N > 1 means S(N) > N, i.e., the sum of three largest proper divisors is greater than N. That is possible (e.g., 12->13>12). T_M < 1 means S(M) < M. So we need N such that S(N) > N, and M = S(N) such that S(M) < M and S(M) = N. So N and M form a 2-cycle.

Now, what about numbers that are not multiples of 12? Could they map to a fixed point in one step? For N to map to a fixed point, we need S(N) ∈ F. We need to find all N (composite with tau>=4) such that S(N) is in F. Then such N are valid a1.

Also, could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in two steps? That is, N such that S(N) is not fixed but S(S(N)) is fixed. Then the sequence would be: N -> M -> M' -> M' -> ... where M' is fixed. For such N to be valid, we need that all intermediate terms have at least 3 proper divisors. So we need to find all N such that iterating S eventually hits F, and all terms in the iteration have tau>=4. This is like the set of numbers that are "good" under S. Similarly, we could have numbers that map to 216, etc. So the set of valid a1 is the set of numbers that are in the "backward orbit" of F under S. That is, all numbers that eventually reach F under iteration of S. But we also need that each term in the iteration has tau>=4. Since F numbers have tau>=4, and if we go backwards, we need to ensure that the numbers we get are also composite with at least 3 proper divisors. That is, they must have tau>=4. So the set S of "good" numbers is the set of numbers that are either in F or can be reached by iterating S backwards from F, i.e., numbers that eventually map into F. But we need to be careful: If a number N is such that S(N) is a fixed point, then N is good. If S(N) is a number that is good (i.e., eventually leads to F), then N is also good, provided N itself has at least 3 proper divisors. So the set of all numbers that eventually reach F under S is the set of all numbers that are in the forward orbit of the "basin of attraction" of F. Since S is not necessarily injective, the backward orbit could be infinite. But the problem asks: "Determine all possible values of a1." So we need to characterize all positive integers a1 such that the infinite sequence defined by the rule exists (i.e., each a_n has at least three proper divisors). That is equivalent to: The sequence a1, a2, a3,... defined by a_{n+1}=S(a_n) is such that all terms are composite with at least three proper divisors. So a1 must be such that the entire forward orbit stays in the set of numbers with tau>=4. In particular, a1 must itself have tau>=4, and S(a1) must have tau>=4, etc.

Thus we need to find all numbers N such that iterating S never produces a number with tau<4. That is, the orbit never hits a prime or a prime square.

We have already found some numbers: F (fixed points) themselves; numbers that map to F in one step (preimages of F). Also, possibly numbers that map to those preimages, etc.

So d2 is the smallest odd prime factor greater than 3, or possibly 9 if 3^2 divides N and no other prime <9? But 5 and 7 are primes less than 9; if N is divisible by 5 or 7, they would be d2. So d2 is either 5 if 5|N, else 7 if 7|N, else 9 if 3^2|N and 5,7∤N. So d2 can be 5,7,9,11,... Then d3 is the next smallest divisor >d2. So we can compute S(N)=N/3 + N/d2 + N/d3 = N*(1/3+1/d2+1/d3). For S(N)=72, we need N = 72/(1/3+1/d2+1/d3). Since N must be integer and also N is odd and divisible by 3.

We can try possible (d2,d3) that are divisors of N. But this seems messy. However, given the difficulty, maybe there is a simpler approach: Show that the only possible a1 are those that are multiples of 6 and are either fixed points (i.e., not divisible by 4 or 5) or numbers that map to fixed points in one step (i.e., numbers ≡72 mod144 and not divisible by 5). But we also have numbers that map to those numbers, etc. The problem likely expects a description like: a1 can be any positive integer N such that N is divisible by 6 and N ≡ 6 (mod 12) or N ≡ 72 (mod 144)? But we saw that fixed points are N ≡6 mod12 and 5∤N. So that's one set. Also numbers that map directly to fixed points are N ≡72 mod144 and 5∤N. But are there numbers that map to numbers that map to fixed points? For instance, consider N such that S(N)=72. We didn't find any, but maybe there are numbers that map to 72? Let's try to solve S(N)=72 for N with p=2? We already did p=2,3∤N gave no solution. What about p=2,3|N? That gave S(N)=13N/12,31N/30,N. For N=72, that's 78, not 72. For N=45? 45 is not divisible by 2. So no. What about p=3? Possibly N= ? Let's try to find N such that S(N)=72.

From the computed values, we see that for numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, S(N)=N (fixed points) or S(N) maps to fixed points? Let's check N=72: S=78, which is fixed. N=216: S=234, fixed. N=504: S=546, fixed. So these are preimages. Are there numbers that map to 72? We need S(N)=72. We didn't find any in our list up to 1000. Let's check if any N gave S(N)=72? N=130 gave 104, N=120 gave 130, N=84 gave 91, N=90 gave 93, N=36 gave 39, N=30 gave 31, N=18 gave 18, N=42 gave 42, N=54 gave 54, N=66 gave 66, N=78 gave 78, N=102 gave 102, N=114 gave 114, N=126 gave 126, N=138 gave 138, N=150 gave 200? Actually 150 gave 155, N=156 gave 169, N=162 gave 162, N=168 gave 182, N=174 gave 174, N=180 gave 195, N=186 gave 186, N=192 gave 208, N=198 gave 198, N=204 gave 221, N=210 gave 217, N=216 gave 234, N=222 gave 222, N=228 gave 247, N=234 gave 234, N=240 gave 260, N=246 gave 246, N=252 gave 273, N=258 gave 258, N=264 gave 286, N=270 gave 279, N=276 gave 287, N=282 gave 282, N=288 gave 288, N=294 gave 287? Wait, 294 gave 287? Actually 294: proper 147,98,42 sum=287. So no. N=300 gave 325, N=306 gave 306, N=312 gave 308? Actually 312: proper 156,104,48 sum=308. N=318 gave 318, N=324 gave 324, N=330 gave 341, N=336 gave 328, N=342 gave 342, N=348 gave 287? Actually 348: proper 174,116,87 sum=277? Let's not.

So it seems that S(N)=72 might not occur for any N up to 1000. But we need to be sure if there is any N that maps to 72. Since we have not found any, and the problem likely expects a description like: a1 can be any positive integer that is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, or any positive integer that is ≡72 mod144 and not divisible by 5, etc. But also numbers that map to those numbers? That would be numbers N such that S(N) is a fixed point, and then S(S(N)) is also fixed point (since fixed point), so N is valid if S(N) is a fixed point. So the set of valid a1 is exactly the set of numbers N for which S(N) is a fixed point (i.e., S(N) ∈ F) and N itself has at least 3 proper divisors. But also, what about numbers that are fixed points themselves? They satisfy S(N)=N∈F, so they are included. So the condition is: S(N) ∈ F. That is the condition for the sequence to continue indefinitely from N, because after one step, we hit a fixed point, and then constant. Could there be numbers that map to a number that is not fixed but then eventually reach a fixed point in more than one step? For example, suppose N is such that S(N)=M, where M is not a fixed point, but S(M)=K, where K is a fixed point. Then the sequence would be N->M->K->K->... So if M is not a fixed point, but S(M) is a fixed point, then N is valid as long as M itself has at least 3 proper divisors (which it does if it's not prime or prime square) and also N itself has at least 3 proper divisors. So we need to consider numbers that are preimages of preimages of F. That is, numbers N such that S(N) is a preimage of F (i.e., S(S(N)) ∈ F). So the set of valid a1 is the union of all backward orbits of F under S. That is, all numbers that eventually reach F under iteration of S.

Thus the problem reduces to characterizing the set of numbers that eventually reach F under the function S. This is an interesting dynamical system. Possibly the only numbers that can appear are those that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5? But we saw that 12 is multiple of 6 but divisible by 4, and it does not map to a fixed point (it maps to 13 prime). So 12 is not in the basin. But could 12 eventually reach F if we go further? 12->13 (prime), so fails. So not. What about 30? 30->31 prime, fails. 36->39->17 prime, fails. 48->52->43 prime, fails. 60->65->19 prime, fails. 84->91->25->? 91->25? 25 has only 2 proper divisors, so fails. 90->93->61 prime, fails. 96->104->91? 104->91? Actually 104: proper 52,26,13 sum=91, 91->25? 91=7*13, proper 13,7,1 sum=21? Actually 91: divisors 1,7,13,91. Three largest proper:13,7,1 sum=21, 21=3*7, proper 7,3,1 sum=11 prime, fails. So many numbers that are not in F lead to primes quickly.

Maybe the basin of F consists exactly of numbers that are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5? But we saw 12 is multiple of 6 and not divisible by 5, but it fails. So that's not true. Wait, 12 is divisible by 4, but it is a multiple of 6? 12/6=2, yes. And it is not divisible by 5. But S(12)=13 prime, so 12 does not map to a fixed point. But could 12 be in the basin if we consider more steps? No, because it hits a prime immediately. So 12 is not in the basin.

But we also have numbers like 18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126, etc. Those are multiples of 6 and not divisible by 5, and they are fixed points. So they are in F. Also numbers like 72,216,504 are multiples of 6 and divisible by 4, but they are not divisible by 5, and they map to fixed points. So they are in the basin. Are there numbers that are multiples of 6, divisible by 4, not divisible by 5, but not of the form 72 mod144? For instance, N=144? 144 is multiple of 12, but is it in the basin? 144->? 144=2^4*3^2, S=72+48+36=156, 156->? 156=2^2*3*13, S=78+52+39=169, 169=13^2, proper:13,1 (2) fails. So 144 not in basin. N=168? 168->? 168=2^3*3*7, S=84+56+42=182, 182->? 182=2*7*13, S=91+26+14=131 prime, fails. N=180? 180->195, 195->? 195=3*5*13, S=65+39+15=119, 119=7*17, S=17+7+1=25, 25->? 25 fails. So 180 not. N=192? 192->208, 208->? 208=2^4*13, S=104+52+26=182, 182->131 prime, fails. So not.

What about N=200? 200->? 200=2^3*5^2, S=100+50+40=190, 190=2*5*19, S=95+38+19=152, 152=8*19, S=76+38+19=133, 133=7*19, S=19+7+1=27, 27=3^3, S=9+3+1=13 prime, fails. So not.

What about N=216? Works. So the pattern might be that the only numbers that map to fixed points are those that are multiples of 12 and of the form 12k where k ≡6 mod12? Actually 72=12*6, 216=12*18, 504=12*42, 648=12*54, 720? 720=12*60, but 720->? 720=2^4*3^2*5, S=360+240+144=744, 744->? 744=2^3*3*31, S=372+248+124=744? Actually 744 is fixed? 744=2^3*3*31, S=372+248+124=744, yes fixed? 372+248+124=744, yes. So 720 maps to 744 which is fixed? Check 744: divisors? 744=2^3*3*31. Smallest:2,3,4? 4 divides 744? 744/4=186, yes. So three smallest:2,3,4. S=744/2+744/3+744/4=372+248+186=806, not 744. Wait, I need to recompute: For N=744, p=2, d2=3, e=4? Since 4 divides 744, yes. So S=744/2+744/3+744/4=372+248+186=806. So 744 is not fixed. But we thought 744 might be fixed? Let's check: 744=2^3*3*31. Divisors: 1,2,3,4,6,8,12,24,31,62,93,124,186,248,372,744. Three largest proper: 372,248,186? Sum=806. So S(744)=806. So not fixed. But we previously computed S(720)=744? Let's compute 720: 720=2^4*3^2*5. Smallest:2,3,4? 4 divides 720? Yes. So S=720/2+720/3+720/4=360+240+180=780. Not 744. I miscomputed earlier. Let's recompute 720: divisors >1:2,3,4,5,6,8,9,10,12,15,16,18,20,24,30,36,40,45,48,60,72,80,90,96,120,144,180,240,360,720. Three largest proper:360,240,180 sum=780. So S(720)=780. 780=2^2*3*5*13, S=390+260+156=806? Actually 780/2=390, 780/3=260, 780/4=195? Wait, need three largest: 390,260,195 sum=845. Not fixed. So 720 not in basin. So the only numbers we have found in basin are those that map directly to fixed points (like 72,216,504,648, etc.) and the fixed points themselves. Also, could there be numbers that map to 72? That would be numbers N such that S(N)=72. We haven't found any up to 1000. Let's try to solve S(N)=72 systematically. Since S(N) is the sum of three largest proper divisors, which is N/p + N/d + N/e. So N = 72 / (1/p+1/d+1/e). Since 1/p+1/d+1/e is a rational number with denominator dividing something. For N to be integer, (1/p+1/d+1/e) must be of the form 72/N. Also p,d,e are divisors of N. This is a bit of a diophantine puzzle.

We can try to find all possible triples (p,d,e) such that the sum of reciprocals is less than 1? Actually 72/N could be >1 if N<72. But N must be composite with tau>=4. For N<72, we can list possible N and compute S(N) to see if any give 72. We can try to analyze the structure of S(N) more generally. Let's denote the three smallest divisors >1 as p, d, e. Then S(N) = N/p + N/d + N/e = N * T, where T = 1/p+1/d+1/e. Thus no N gives S(N)=72. So 72 has no preimage. Similarly, for any M that is a preimage of a fixed point (i.e., M ≡72 mod144 and 5∤M), can we have S(N)=M? That would require N such that sum of three largest proper divisors = M. Since M is at least 72, we can try to show that if M is a preimage of a fixed point, then M is not a sum of three largest proper divisors of any integer. That is, the set of numbers that are sums of three largest proper divisors is exactly the set of numbers that are either prime? Actually we need to show that no number of the form 72 mod144, not divisible by 5, can be expressed as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of some integer. That seems plausible but we need a proof.

However, maybe we don't need to show that there are no further preimages. The problem asks: Determine all possible values of a1. So we need to describe the set S of numbers a1 such that the sequence is defined infinitely. We have shown that if a1 ∈ F (fixed points), then constant sequence works. If a1 is a preimage of F under S (i.e., S(a1) ∈ F), then after one step we hit a fixed point, and then constant. So these are valid. Are there any other numbers that eventually reach F after more than one step? That would mean that there exists some n>1 such that a_n ∈ F, and all terms before that have at least 3 proper divisors. But we need to consider that the sequence may eventually become periodic with a cycle that does not include a fixed point. Could there be a cycle of length >1? For example, could there be distinct numbers N and M such that S(N)=M and S(M)=N? Then the sequence would oscillate between N and M. For this to be valid, both N and M must have at least three proper divisors. We need to examine if such 2-cycles exist. Let's try to find possible 2-cycles. If S(N)=M and S(M)=N, then N = S(M) = M/p_M + M/d_M + M/e_M, and M = S(N) = N/p_N + N/d_N + N/e_N. So N = M * T_M, M = N * T_N, with T_M = 1/p_M+1/d_M+1/e_M, T_N = 1/p_N+1/d_N+1/e_N. Then N = M * T_M = (N * T_N) * T_M => 1 = T_N * T_M. So T_N * T_M = 1. Since T_N and T_M are positive rationals. For their product to be 1, either both are 1 (giving fixed points) or one is >1 and the other <1. So we need a pair of numbers N and M such that T_N > 1 and T_M < 1, and T_N * T_M = 1. In particular, T_N > 1 means that the sum of reciprocals of the three smallest divisors of N is greater than 1. That would mean that N has a relatively small set of divisors such that the reciprocals sum >1. For example, if p=2 and d=3 and e=4, then T=13/12 >1. If p=2 and d=3 and e=6, then T=1. If p=2 and d=4 and e=5, then T=19/20 <1. So possible T values are rational numbers. For T_N * T_M =1, we need T_N = 1/T_M. So if T_N >1, then T_M <1. So we need a pair of numbers where the T-values are reciprocals.

We can try to see if any such pair exists. Suppose N has T_N = 13/12 (i.e., N is multiple of 12 with 3,4,? Actually T=13/12 occurs when p=2,d=3,e=4, i.e., N is multiple of 12, and also 4|N? Actually T=13/12 occurs when the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4. That requires N divisible by 2,3,4. So N is multiple of 12. Then T_N=13/12. Then we need T_M = 12/13. So we need a number M such that the sum of reciprocals of its three smallest divisors is 12/13. That is, 1/p+1/d+1/e = 12/13. Since 12/13 <1, it's possible. Solve for integers p,d,e. 1/p+1/d+1/e = 12/13. Since p is prime, and 12/13 ≈0.923, the reciprocals sum is close to 1. The smallest possible p is 2, then 1/2=0.5, so we need 1/d+1/e = 12/13 - 1/2 = (24-13)/26 = 11/26 ≈0.423. The sum of reciprocals of two integers >2. The possible pairs (d,e) with d<e. For d=3, 1/3≈0.333, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/3 = (33-26)/78 = 7/78 ≈0.0897 => e=78/7≈11.14, not integer. d=4, 1/4=0.25, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/4 = (44-26)/104 = 18/104 = 9/52 => e=52/9≈5.78, no. d=5, 1/5=0.2, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/5 = (55-26)/130 = 29/130 => e=130/29≈4.48, no. d=6, 1/6≈0.1667, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/6 = (66-26)/156 = 40/156 = 10/39 => e=39/10=3.9, no. d=7, 1/7≈0.1429, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/7 = (77-26)/182 = 51/182 => e=182/51≈3.57, no. d=8, 1/8=0.125, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/8 = (88-26)/208 = 62/208 = 31/104 => e=104/31≈3.35, no. d=9, 1/9≈0.1111, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/9 = (99-26)/234 = 73/234 => e=234/73≈3.21, no. d=10, 1/10=0.1, need 1/e = 11/26 - 0.1 = (55-26)/130 = 29/130 => e=130/29≈4.48, no. d=11, 1/11≈0.0909, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/11 = (121-26)/286 = 95/286 => e=286/95≈3.01, no. d=12, 1/12≈0.0833, need 1/e = 11/26 - 1/12 = (132-26)/312 = 106/312 = 53/156 => e=156/53≈2.94, no. So no solution with p=2. What about p=3? Then T = 1/3+1/d+1/e = 12/13 => 1/d+1/e = 12/13 - 1/3 = (36-13)/39 = 23/39 ≈0.5897. So no. Thus no M with T=12/13.

Therefore, the only possible pair for a 2-cycle would be if T_N and T_M are reciprocals, but we have not found any such pairs. Perhaps we can prove that if T_N >1, then T_N is of the form 1 + 1/d + 1/e, and such T_N is always >1 but less than 2? Actually T_N = 1/p+1/d+1/e. Since p is at least 2, and d,e≥3, the maximum T_N occurs when p=2,d=3,e=4 giving 13/12 ≈1.0833. So T_N can only be 13/12, 1, or less than 1? Actually if p=2,d=3,e=6 gives T=1. If p=2,d=4,e=5 gives T=19/20 <1. So T_N >1 only occurs when p=2,d=3,e=4 (since p=2,d=3,e=4 is the only case where sum >1). For p=2,d=3,e=4, T=13/12. So if T_N >1, then N must be a multiple of 12 with p=2,d=3,e=4. That means N ≡0 mod12, and also not divisible by any divisor between 2 and 3? That's automatic, and also 4 divides N? Actually if N is multiple of 12, then it is divisible by 4? 12=4*3, so yes, 4 divides N. So the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4. So T_N=13/12. Then we need T_M = 12/13. As we saw, T_M = 12/13 would require a solution to 1/p+1/d+1/e = 12/13. We can try to see if any such solution exists. Since 12/13 is close to 1, and p is at least 2. Thus no solution for T_M = 12/13. Hence there is no 2-cycle. Similarly, for longer cycles, we would need T-values that multiply to 1, but the only T>1 is 13/12, and its reciprocal 12/13 is not attainable. So no cycles.

Therefore, the only possible infinite sequences are those that eventually become constant at a fixed point. And we have characterized the numbers that map to fixed points in one step (those with S(N)∈F) and the fixed points themselves. But also, could there be numbers that map to a fixed point in more than one step? That would require a chain N -> M -> K where K∈F and M is not in F, but S(M)∈F. So M is a preimage of F, i.e., M ≡72 mod144 and 5∤M. So we need to check if any N can have S(N)=M where M is such a preimage. That is, we need to see if any number can be expressed as the sum of three largest proper divisors of some integer. We already argued that S(N) for N that is a multiple of 12 is 13N/12. So if we want S(N)=M, we need 13N/12 = M => N = 12M/13. For M ≡72 mod144, M is of the form 72+144k, not divisible by 5. Then 12M/13 = 12(72+144k)/13 = (864+1728k)/13 = 864/13 + 1728k/13. Since 864/13 is not integer, N would not be integer. So no N with p=2,d=3,e=4 gives S(N)=M. What about other structures? Could there be an N such that its three smallest divisors are not 2,3,4 but still S(N) is a number of the form 72+144k? For that, we need to solve S(N)=M. Since S(N) = N/p + N/d + N/e, with p,d,e the smallest divisors. For N to give S(N)=M, we need to find p,d,e such that N = M / T, with T=1/p+1/d+1/e. So we need to find integer solutions to N = M / T. For M large, it's plausible that some N could satisfy this. But we need to check if any such N can have all terms in the sequence with at least 3 proper divisors. But maybe we can prove that if S(N) is not a fixed point, then S(N) must be of the form 13N/12, i.e., N is multiple of 12 with 3,4 as next divisors? Actually we have already classified the possibilities for S(N) based on the prime factor structure. For N even, the second smallest divisor d is either 3 (if 3|N) or 4 (if N divisible by 4 and not by 3). In both cases, S(N) is either N/2+N/3+N/4 =13N/12 (if 3|N and 4|N), or N/2+N/4+N/e = 3N/4+N/e (if 4|N but 3∤N). The latter can be various rational multiples. For S(N) to be a number that is a preimage of a fixed point (i.e., M ≡72 mod144, 5∤M), we can try to see if any such N exists. We already looked at the case p=2,d=4,e=5 gave S=19N/20. For N=20, S=19; not. For N=28, S=25; not. For N=44, S=37; not. For N=52, S=43; not. For N=68, S=55; not. For N=76, S=61; not. For N=92, S=73; not. For N=116, S=91; not. For N=140, S=109; not. For N=148, S=115; not. So none give a number ≡72 mod144. For p=2,d=4,e=7 gave S=25N/28. For N=56, S=50; not. For N=112, S=100; not. For N=224, S=200; not. So no. For p=2,d=4,e=8 gave S=7N/8. For N=56, S=49; not. For N=88, S=77; not. For N=104, S=91; not. For N=152, S=133; not. For N=200, S=175; not. For N=248, S=217; not. For N=296, S=259; not. For N=344, S=301; not. For N=392, S=343; not. For N=440, S=385; not. So no. For p=2,d=4,e=9? But 9 divides N only if 3|N, but then 3 would be second smallest, not 4. So no. For p=2,d=4,e=11? Then S=25N/28? Actually N divisible by 4 and 11, so N multiple of 44. Then S = N/2+N/4+N/11 = (3N/4)+N/11 = (33N+4N)/44 = 37N/44. For N=44, S=37; not. For N=88, S=74; not. For N=132, S=111; not. For N=176, S=148; not. So no.

For p=3 case, we have S(N) = N/3+N/d+N/e. For N divisible by 3 and 5, with 9? We already computed S(N) values. None give a number ≡72 mod144. So it seems unlikely that any N can have S(N) = M where M is a preimage of a fixed point. We could try to prove that if S(N) is not a fixed point, then S(N) cannot be of the form 72+144k with 5∤S(N). But maybe it's easier: We can show that if a1 is not a fixed point, then S(a1) is not a preimage of a fixed point? Actually we need to consider all a1 that eventually reach F. We have found that if a1 ∈ F, then constant. If a1 is such that S(a1) ∈ F, then works. Are there any a1 such that S(a1) is not in F but S(S(a1)) ∈ F? That would mean that a2 = S(a1) is a preimage of F (i.e., a2 ≡72 mod144, 5∤a2), and then a3 ∈ F. So we need to check if any a1 can have S(a1) = M where M ≡72 mod144 and 5∤M. Therefore, the basin of F consists exactly of the fixed points and their preimages. But we should also consider numbers that are themselves preimages of F? That is, numbers that map to F in one step. Those are valid a1. And then numbers that are fixed points are valid. And there are no numbers that map to preimages of F in one step because that would imply that the preimage itself is a sum of three largest proper divisors of some number, which we have argued is not the case. But we need a rigorous proof that there is no integer N such that S(N) = M where M ≡72 mod144 and 5∤M. Perhaps we can prove that if S(N) is not a fixed point, then S(N) must be of the form 13N/12 (if N multiple of 12) or 3N/4+N/e, and in the latter case S(N) is always less than N? Actually for N even but not divisible by 3, we have p=2, d=4, e≥5. Then S(N)=3N/4+N/e. Since N/e ≤ N/5 (because e≥5), we have S(N) ≤ 3N/4+N/5 = (15N+4N)/20=19N/20. So S(N) ≤ 19N/20 < N. So S(N) is less than N. For N even and divisible by 3, we have p=2,d=3,e=4 gives S=13N/12 > N. So the only case where S(N) > N is when N is a multiple of 12 and not divisible by any divisor between 2 and 3? That is automatic. So S(N) can be greater than N only for N that are multiples of 12. For those N, S(N)=13N/12. So if we want S(N)=M with M>N, then we must have M = 13N/12, so N = 12M/13. For M to be a preimage of a fixed point, we need M ≡72 mod144. Then 12M/13 = 12(72+144k)/13 = (864+1728k)/13 = 864/13 + 1728k/13. Since 864/13 is not integer, N is not integer. Hence no N with S(N)=M where M is a preimage of a fixed point. Therefore, the only numbers that can map to a fixed point are those that are multiples of 12 and satisfy N ≡72 mod144. But those are exactly the preimages. So the forward orbit of any number that is not a fixed point either hits a prime/square (fails) or hits a preimage of a fixed point (then next step fixed point). So the set of a1 that work is exactly F ∪ { N : S(N) ∈ F }.

And we already have that if N is a multiple of 12, then S(N)=13N/12 if N is not divisible by 3? Wait, we need to be careful: For N multiple of 12, we have p=2,d=3,e=4? But is it always the case that the three smallest divisors are 2,3,4? For N multiple of 12, it is divisible by 2,3,4. But could there be a divisor between 2 and 3? No. So the second smallest is 3. Could there be a divisor between 3 and 4? No. So the third smallest is 4. So yes, for any N divisible by 12, the three smallest divisors are exactly 2,3,4. Thus S(N)=N/2+N/3+N/4=13N/12. So for N multiple of 12, S(N)=13N/12. Therefore, the condition S(N) ∈ F becomes 13N/12 ∈ F. So we need 13N/12 ≡6 mod12 and 5∤13N/12. This gives N ≡72 mod144 and 5∤N. So the set of N with S(N)∈F is exactly { N : N ≡72 mod144, 5∤N }. But note that N ≡72 mod144 implies N divisible by 12? 72 mod144 is 72, which is divisible by 12. Yes, 72/12=6. So N is a multiple of 12. And also N ≡72 mod144 gives N=72+144k. Then 5∤N? That's an extra condition. So the preimages of F are exactly numbers N such that N ≡72 (mod144) and 5∤N.

But could there be N that are multiples of 12 but with S(N) ∈ F due to other structures? No, because S(N) is always 13N/12 for multiples of 12. So that's it.

Thus the set of a1 that work is F ∪ { N : N ≡72 mod144 and 5∤N }.

Now, we need to describe these sets in a clean way. Perhaps we can combine them: a1 is a positive integer such that either a1 ≡6 mod12 and 5∤a1, or a1 ≡72 mod144 and 5∤a1. But note that numbers ≡6 mod12 and not divisible by 5 include numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,... Some of these are also ≡72 mod144? For example, 6 is not 72 mod144. 18 not. 42 not. 54 not. 66 not. 78 not. 102 not. So the two sets are disjoint? Check: 72 mod144 is 72,216,360,504,... Among these, 216 is 216 mod12=0, so not in F because F requires ≡6 mod12. 360 mod12=0, so not. 504 mod12=0, so not. So indeed the two sets are disjoint. So the possible a1 are all numbers that are multiples of 12 and satisfy a1 ≡72 mod144 and 5∤a1, or numbers that are multiples of 6 but not of 12? Actually F is numbers ≡6 mod12 and 5∤N. That includes numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138, etc. But note that 12 is not in F. So F numbers are exactly numbers that are 6 mod12 and not divisible by 5. So we can write the answer as: a1 can be any positive integer N such that N is divisible by 6 and either N ≡6 (mod12) and 5∤N, or N ≡0 (mod12) and N ≡72 (mod144) and 5∤N. But since N ≡72 mod144 implies N ≡0 mod12, we can combine: N is a multiple of 6 and either N ≡6 mod12 and 5∤N, or N ≡72 mod144 and 5∤N.

But is there any number that is a multiple of 6 and ≡6 mod12? Those are exactly numbers that are 6 mod12. And numbers that are multiples of 12 and ≡72 mod144 are numbers that are 72 mod144. So the answer can be: a1 can be any positive integer N such that N is divisible by 6 and N ≡6 (mod12) or N ≡72 (mod144), with the additional condition that 5 does not divide N. However, note that if N ≡6 mod12, then N is not divisible by 12? Actually 6 mod12 means N=6,18,30,42,54,66,78,90,102,114,126,138,150,... Among these, those that are multiples of 5 are excluded. So 30,90,150,... are excluded. So the condition 5∤N is necessary for both families. But is it automatically satisfied for N ≡72 mod144? 72 mod144 is 72,216,360,504,648,792,936,1080, etc. Are any of these divisible by 5? 72 no, 216 no, 360 yes (360/5=72), so 360 is divisible by 5, so excluded. So indeed we need to explicitly exclude multiples of 5.

Thus the set of possible a1 is: { N ∈ ℕ : 6|N, 5∤N, and either N ≡6 (mod12) or N ≡72 (mod144) }.
